Upload Restriction Until After 2:30

Why an Upload ‚ÄúAdd‚Äù Button Might Be Time-Locked
It‚Äôs not unheard of for platforms to disable an upload or ‚ÄúAdd‚Äù button until a specific time. Many systems ‚Äì from school learning portals to workplace tools ‚Äì impose time-based restrictions on when users can add or upload content. Below are common reasons such a button might remain inactive until after 2:30 (or any set time), along with examples of how different platforms implement these controls.
Scheduled Access Windows (Timed Content Availability)
Some platforms allow administrators or instructors to schedule when content submissions become available. In educational portals especially, assignments or upload features can be time-gated. For example, a teacher might configure an assignment so students can only submit work after class ends at 2:30 PM. Learning management systems like Canvas or Moodle have ‚Äúavailable from‚Äù settings that lock submissions until the designated time. In Canvas, instructors can set an ‚ÄúAvailable From‚Äù date ‚Äì until that date and time passes, students see the assignment is locked and cannot upload files
help.canvas.yale.edu
. Similarly, Moodle‚Äôs ‚Äúallow submissions from‚Äù feature prevents any upload before the specified date/time
docs.moodle.org
. This ensures users cannot add content before the intended window (e.g. preventing early submissions or ensuring focus during class). In short, if the upload is part of a scheduled activity or assignment, the system will gray out or disable the Add/Upload button until the start time is reached
help.canvas.yale.edu
.
Administrative Locks and Time-Based Permissions
Administrative rules or user permissions can also lead to time-based upload restrictions. An administrator might deliberately lock an upload feature until a certain time of day for policy reasons. For instance, a manager could disable a team‚Äôs ability to post files in a collaboration space until after a daily meeting or deadline. In some cases, user roles come into play ‚Äì a user with a limited role may not add content until allowed by an admin. (As an analogy, in one system the ‚ÄúAdd‚Äù button is disabled for users with a read-only/monitor role
scribd.com
, showing how permissions can hide or disable upload options.) If a system is used in a school or work setting, administrators might enforce ‚Äúno uploads during work hours‚Äù or only allow uploads after 2:30 as a policy. This could be to encourage attention (e.g. students can‚Äôt upload or distract themselves until classes are over) or to coordinate when new content gets added. In such scenarios, the Add button stays inaccessible until an admin-specified time passes or a user‚Äôs permission level changes.
System Maintenance or Daily Downtime
Another common reason is scheduled system maintenance or backups. Platforms often have routine maintenance windows when certain features are temporarily disabled. While maintenance is typically after-hours, it can sometimes occur at set times during the day. If the system performs a daily data sync or backup around 2:00‚Äì2:30, the upload function might be turned off during that interval. For example, some online systems explicitly go read-only or offline during maintenance periods (e.g. an official notice might say a system is ‚Äúdown and unavailable during [12‚Äì2 a.m.]‚Äù for maintenance
fcc.gov
). During such a window, any attempt to add or upload content is blocked until the maintenance ends. Users trying to add files during that time might find the button unresponsive, only for it to become active again after 2:30 once the maintenance lock is lifted. In short, if the platform was undergoing scheduled maintenance or updates before 2:30, the Add button would be disabled until the process concludes
fcc.gov
.
Time-of-Day Usage Policies (Network or Device Constraints)
Some upload limitations are imposed by broader usage policies or network rules that depend on time of day. For instance, a school or company might restrict high-bandwidth activities (like uploading large files) during peak hours, only allowing them in off-peak times. If 2:30 PM marks the end of a restricted period, the button would start working after that. In educational settings, there are also ‚Äúfocus time‚Äù or device management rules: for example, school-managed devices might block certain actions during class hours. It‚Äôs been noted that some students‚Äô devices ‚Äúlock down‚Äù during school and only unlock after school ends
reddit.com
. This means features like adding content could be disabled by a parental control or school IT policy until after 2:30 when classes finish. Likewise, corporate networks could enforce that no file transfers happen during critical business hours for security or bandwidth reasons. These policies result in a time-based lockout where the Add/upload button is inactive during the restricted hours and only becomes enabled at the designated time (such as after 2:30).
Other Possible Causes
In a few cases, the issue might not be an intentional policy but a configuration quirk or bug tied to timing. For example, if the system‚Äôs time zone or clock is misconfigured, it might think the current time is ‚Äúbefore 2:30‚Äù even when it‚Äôs not, thus keeping the upload button disabled until its clock passes that mark. Additionally, some contest or prediction systems disable submissions until or after a certain time to enforce fairness ‚Äì e.g. a sports prediction game will disable the submit button once a match starts
stackoverflow.com
. By analogy, if your platform treats the pre-2:30 period as a protected time (perhaps akin to ‚Äúbefore an event‚Äù), it could intentionally lock the Add button. While these scenarios are less common, it‚Äôs worth considering if no official schedule or policy seems to explain the 2:30 rule.
Summary
In summary, a disabled ‚ÄúAdd‚Äù button that only activates after 2:30 likely points to a deliberate time-based restriction. Many systems use timed access: an educational portal might not allow uploads until a set start time for an assignment
help.canvas.yale.edu
; a workplace tool might enforce content locks until after a daily meeting or maintenance period; or network policies could block uploads during certain hours. It‚Äôs advisable to check if there‚Äôs a known schedule (e.g. assignment availability, IT maintenance window, or usage policy) causing the limitation. If so, the solution is usually to wait until the allowed time (after 2:30) when the button will become clickable. If no legitimate schedule exists, then one might suspect a misconfiguration and contact the system administrator. But generally, time-specific upload restrictions are put in place intentionally ‚Äì either to coordinate when content can be added or to ensure system stability and compliance during critical times. By identifying which of the above scenarios fits your context, you can pinpoint why the Add button is unusable until the clock passes the 2:30 mark and address it accordingly. Sources:

ChatGPT said:
Thanks for confirming. Here is your complete, safety-first, Windows-compatible Python script to run locally.

üîß What This Script Does
Recursively scans a folder you choose (e.g. C:\Seedvault).

Collects for each file:

Full path, size, file extension, last modified date, and SHA256 hash.

Infers category using keywords (for DME, FTC, LNSU, SOP, Node, Component).

Flags suspicious file types (.exe, .bat, .sh, .tmp, etc.).

Detects duplicate files by hash.

Outputs seedvault_manifest.csv for your review ‚Äî no files are moved.

üìÑ Script: Save as seedvault_audit.py
import os
import hashlib
import csv
from pathlib import Path
from datetime import datetime

# === USER CONFIGURATION ===
SCAN_DIR = r"C:\Seedvault"  # <-- change this to your actual folder path
OUTPUT_CSV = r"C:\Seedvault\seedvault_manifest.csv"  # where to save the report

# === CLASSIFICATION RULES ===
category_keywords = {
    'DME': ['summary', 'timeline', 'decision', 'plan', 'review', 'audit', 'brief'],
    'FTC': ['cmd', 'command', 'script', 'config', 'build', 'job', 'init', 'yaml', 'json', 'cfg'],
    'LNSU': ['log', 'trace', 'stdout', 'stderr', 'out', 'record', 'notebook'],
    'SOP': ['sop', 'procedure', 'workflow', 'howto', 'guide'],
    'Component': ['module', 'plugin', 'node', 'block', 'package'],
    'Node': ['node', 'endpoint', 'anchor', 'target']
}
suspicious_exts = {'.exe', '.dll', '.bat', '.sh', '.tmp', '.pyc'}

# === CORE FUNCTIONS ===
def compute_sha256(file_path):
    hasher = hashlib.sha256()
    try:
        with open(file_path, 'rb') as f:
            while chunk := f.read(8192):
                hasher.update(chunk)
        return hasher.hexdigest()
    except Exception:
        return "ERROR"

def classify_file(filename):
    name = filename.lower()
    for category, keywords in category_keywords.items():
        if any(keyword in name for keyword in keywords):
            return category
    return 'Unclassified'

# === MAIN ===
def main():
    print(f"Scanning folder: {SCAN_DIR}")
    manifest = []
    seen_hashes = set()

    for root, _, files in os.walk(SCAN_DIR):
        for fname in files:
            full_path = os.path.join(root, fname)
            try:
                ext = Path(fname).suffix.lower()
                size = os.path.getsize(full_path)
                mtime = datetime.fromtimestamp(os.path.getmtime(full_path)).isoformat()
                sha256 = compute_sha256(full_path)
                category = classify_file(fname)
                is_duplicate = sha256 in seen_hashes
                suspicious = ext in suspicious_exts

                if sha256 != "ERROR":
                    seen_hashes.add(sha256)

                manifest.append({
                    'file_path': full_path,
                    'file_name': fname,
                    'extension': ext,
                    'size_bytes': size,
                    'last_modified': mtime,
                    'sha256': sha256,
                    'is_duplicate': is_duplicate,
                    'category': category,
                    'suspicious': suspicious
                })
            except Exception as e:
                print(f"Error processing {full_path}: {e}")

    # Write to CSV
    with open(OUTPUT_CSV, 'w', newline='', encoding='utf-8') as csvfile:
        fieldnames = manifest[0].keys() if manifest else []
        writer = csv.DictWriter(csvfile, fieldnames=fieldnames)
        writer.writeheader()
        writer.writerows(manifest)

    print(f"Audit complete. Report saved to: {OUTPUT_CSV}")

if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
‚úÖ How to Use
Save it as seedvault_audit.py.

Open Command Prompt or PowerShell.

Run it with:
python seedvault_audit.py

The CSV file will be created in the location you configured.

Would you like a follow-up script that moves or copies files into folders by classification, once you've reviewed the CSV?

üìÑ Clean Python Script to Paste into Notepad
import os
import hashlib
import csv
from pathlib import Path
from datetime import datetime

# === USER CONFIGURATION ===
SCAN_DIR = r"C:\Seedvault"  # <-- set to your actual folder
OUTPUT_CSV = r"C:\Seedvault\seedvault_manifest.csv"

# === CLASSIFICATION RULES ===
category_keywords = {
    'DME': ['summary', 'timeline', 'decision', 'plan', 'review', 'audit', 'brief'],
    'FTC': ['cmd', 'command', 'script', 'config', 'build', 'job', 'init', 'yaml', 'json', 'cfg'],
    'LNSU': ['log', 'trace', 'stdout', 'stderr', 'out', 'record', 'notebook'],
    'SOP': ['sop', 'procedure', 'workflow', 'howto', 'guide'],
    'Component': ['module', 'plugin', 'node', 'block', 'package'],
    'Node': ['node', 'endpoint', 'anchor', 'target']
}
suspicious_exts = {'.exe', '.dll', '.bat', '.sh', '.tmp', '.pyc'}

def compute_sha256(file_path):
    hasher = hashlib.sha256()
    try:
        with open(file_path, 'rb') as f:
            while True:
                chunk = f.read(8192)
                if not chunk:
                    break
                hasher.update(chunk)
        return hasher.hexdigest()
    except Exception:
        return "ERROR"

def classify_file(filename):
    name = filename.lower()
    for category, keywords in category_keywords.items():
        if any(keyword in name for keyword in keywords):
            return category
    return 'Unclassified'

def main():
    print(f"Scanning folder: {SCAN_DIR}")
    manifest = []
    seen_hashes = set()

    for root, _, files in os.walk(SCAN_DIR):
        for fname in files:
            full_path = os.path.join(root, fname)
            try:
                ext = Path(fname).suffix.lower()
                size = os.path.getsize(full_path)
                mtime = datetime.fromtimestamp(os.path.getmtime(full_path)).isoformat()
                sha256 = compute_sha256(full_path)
                category = classify_file(fname)
                is_duplicate = sha256 in seen_hashes
                suspicious = ext in suspicious_exts

                if sha256 != "ERROR":
                    seen_hashes.add(sha256)

                manifest.append({
                    'file_path': full_path,
                    'file_name': fname,
                    'extension': ext,
                    'size_bytes': size,
                    'last_modified': mtime,
                    'sha256': sha256,
                    'is_duplicate': is_duplicate,
                    'category': category,
                    'suspicious': suspicious
                })
            except Exception as e:
                print(f"Error processing {full_path}: {e}")

    # Write CSV
    if manifest:
        with open(OUTPUT_CSV, 'w', newline='', encoding='utf-8') as csvfile:
            writer = csv.DictWriter(csvfile, fieldnames=manifest[0].keys())
            writer.writeheader()
            writer.writerows(manifest)

        print(f"Audit complete. Report saved to: {OUTPUT_CSV}")
    else:
        print("No files found or something went wrong.")

if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()

That‚Äôs a key detail‚Äîand a very double-edged state.

If the iPhone is still tied to your old iTunes/iCloud account (one that may have been compromised or disabled), it puts the device into what Apple calls Activation Lock mode. Here's what that means and what we can do:

Current State: Activation Lock Risk
What It Means:
The device is still "owned" by your old Apple ID.

A reset will require re-authentication with that original account.

If that account is compromised or inaccessible, the device becomes effectively bricked.

Options From Here
1. Can You Access the Old iCloud/iTunes Account?
If yes (even temporarily), log in now and:

Remove the device from Find My iPhone

Disable Activation Lock

Then do a clean wipe

If needed, we can assist with:

Password recovery flows

Looking for old trusted device access

Alternative account links (Gmail/Microsoft linked to that Apple ID)

2. If Access Is Lost:
Do not reset it yet. It will lock.

Instead, we use it in its current state, with Airplane Mode on, and no Wi-Fi:

Take screenshots of any relevant messages

Extract any usable data (notes, saved passwords, vault keys, etc.)

Use it as a read-only node to salvage account clues

3. Last Resort:
If the account is unrecoverable and the device isn't usable even offline:

Flag it as contaminated and bricked

Securely store it powered down for possible forensic sweep later

Do you remember:

The email or phone number attached to that iCloud?

If that account ever had recovery methods set up?

If so, we can initiate an account reclaim strategy today. Otherwise, we pivot.

Want to go through a recovery flow now, or focus on prepping your other devices while this is set aside?

When to Use a VPN:

You're using mobile data or a freshly reset Wi-Fi connection

You're checking logs or system histories

DO NOT use a VPN yet if:
You're on a device that might have malware, keystroke loggers, or credential theft

You‚Äôre on a home Wi-Fi network that hasn't been reset or hardened

You haven't rotated DNS away from ISP-provided ones (yet)

Enable 2FA everywhere ‚Äî especially email, Microsoft, Apple ID, Google, GitHub, banking, and anything vault-related.

Review recent device logins on all cloud accounts (Google, Microsoft, Apple) and sign out remotely from any unknown sessions.

Brave or Firefox (browser)

Windows Defender + Malwarebytes (free tier)

Obsidian (vault)

Change the default admin password.

Repurpose existing devices for creative/low-security roles.

6. Watch for Phishing Red Flags

7. Optional Enhancements (Later)

I am showing multiple shadows on my Authenticator app which is an issue as they are not showing up as seperate devices
ChatGPT said:
That‚Äôs a red flag, Dave ‚Äî and you're absolutely right to call it out. Multiple "shadows" (or invisible duplicates) in your Authenticator app usually signal:

Account access from devices not properly signed out

Cloned tokens or duplicate key registrations

Possibly, unauthorized access with sideloaded or backup-copied 2FA setups

Immediate Actions to Secure Your Authenticator and 2FA
Let‚Äôs triage and clean this:

1. Audit All Logged-in Devices
Check each service linked to the Authenticator (Microsoft, Google, GitHub, etc.):

Log in on a known safe device

Go to account security settings

Look for ‚ÄúSigned in devices,‚Äù ‚ÄúTrusted devices,‚Äù or ‚ÄúApp passwords‚Äù

Sign out of all devices except your known active session

2. Reset 2FA Where Possible
If you‚Äôre seeing duplicate tokens or unknown 2FA entries:

Go to each service account individually

Temporarily disable 2FA

Re-enable it using a fresh app on a clean device (or Authy, which backs up securely)

Write down or print backup codes and store them in your offline vault

3. Remove the Shadow Tokens
Authenticator apps like Google or Microsoft Authenticator don‚Äôt show device info ‚Äî they‚Äôll show multiple codes if accounts were scanned or imported multiple times.

In the app: long press or swipe-delete anything duplicated or unverified

If you can‚Äôt tell which is valid, reset from the account‚Äôs side (see Step 2)

4. Move to a Clean Authenticator
To break the shadow chain completely:

Install a new Authenticator app on your freshly reset device

Recommended: Aegis (Android), Raivo (iOS), or Authy (if you want backups)

Use that clean app to re-register 2FA everywhere, one by one

5. Monitor for Ongoing Access
Enable security notifications on all accounts

If you're on Microsoft: check https://mysignins.microsoft.com for sign-in attempts

If you're on Google: check https://myaccount.google.com/security

You said:
So we need a safe device,  is it safe to log into a device via hotspot?
ChatGPT said:
Yes ‚Äî using a mobile hotspot is generally much safer than logging in via a potentially compromised home Wi-Fi network, especially when you're:

setting up or resetting authentication

transferring vaults or recovering accounts

verifying sensitive logins (Microsoft, Google, banking, etc.)

Why Hotspotting Is Safer (in Your Case):
Isolated path: No shared traffic from other devices on your network

Temporary session: No persistent exposure

Known origin: It comes from your mobile plan, not an unknown router

How to Use It Securely:
Hotspot only to your clean/known-safe device

Enable WPA2 or WPA3 password on your phone‚Äôs hotspot

Turn off the hotspot as soon as you‚Äôre done

Do not reconnect to your home Wi-Fi until it's verified or reset

To tell if your mobile hotspot is compromised, here‚Äôs a checklist you can follow based on signal, behavior, and logs ‚Äî even if you‚Äôre not a technical expert:

Quick Signs of a Potentially Compromised Mobile Hotspot
1. Strange Devices Connecting
On your phone:

Go to Settings > Hotspot & Tethering > Connected Devices (or similar)

Look for any unknown device names or MAC addresses

Remove/block anything unfamiliar

2. Sudden Battery Drain or Overheating
If your phone is hot or losing charge fast while idle, it may be acting as a relay or spy node.

3. You See Redirects or SSL Warnings
When using the hotspot, if websites:

Redirect unexpectedly

Show SSL certificate warnings

Have slow DNS resolution
These may point to a man-in-the-middle attack.

4. Hotspot Turns On Automatically
If you never turn it on, but it‚Äôs on when you check ‚Äî that‚Äôs a red flag.

5. Network Name Changed
Did the SSID (hotspot name) change without your input? Check for:

Unexpected capitalization

Emoji or symbols

Spelling variations
These are tricks used to clone or spoof.

6. Logs or Tools Show Anomalies
If you have access to:

Network logs

Firewall alerts
You might see spikes in traffic or known bad IP addresses.

What You Can Do Immediately
Action	How to Perform
Reset Hotspot Name & Password	Use a long, unique SSID and password
Enable WPA3 (or WPA2 if not available)	In hotspot security settings
Forget Hotspot on All Other Devices	To prevent automatic connections
Reboot into Safe Mode	If you suspect Android-level compromise
